Source: Architectural Evolution of Distributed SQL and Cloud-Native Vector Systems: Integrating Distributed SQL, Vector Search Engines, and Heterogeneous Hardware Acceleration
Type: Pasted Text
Architectural Evolution of Distributed SQL and Cloud-Native Vector Systems: Integrating Distributed SQL, Vector Search Engines, and Heterogeneous Hardware AccelerationThe landscape of enterprise data architecture is undergoing a structural realignment driven by the consolidation of transactional and analytical pipelines and the explosive growth of high-dimensional vector embeddings generated by deep learning models.[1, 2, 3] Historically, corporate data stacks were severely fragmented.[4] Transactional systems (OLTP) were physically isolated from analytical platforms (OLAP), necessitating brittle, high-latency Extract-Transform-Load (ETL) pipelines to bridge the divide.[4] The introduction of modern artificial intelligence and machine learning applications has added a third layer of complexity, demanding sub-10 millisecond similarity search across billions of high-dimensional vector embeddings.[3, 5, 6]To resolve these architectural tensions, database engineering has branched into two main paradigms: consolidated NewSQL Hybrid Transactional/Analytical Processing (HTAP) engines with integrated vector capabilities, and dedicated cloud-native vector databases engineered for extreme, highly elastic scale.[1, 3, 7] This study examines the underlying mechanics, storage layouts, query execution pathways, and hardware acceleration protocols of these two paradigms, focusing on TiDB and Milvus/Zilliz Cloud as representative systems.[1, 3, 8]
Consolidated NewSQL Foundations: TiDB Distributed Architecture and TransactionsTiDB is designed as a distributed, cloud-native NewSQL database that decouples compute from storage to facilitate independent, horizontal scaling.[1, 9] This separation of concerns allows each tier to scale independently based on the application's specific CPU, memory, and disk requirements, preventing resource bottlenecks and optimizing total cost of ownership.[1, 9, 10]The physical topology of a TiDB cluster is organized into independent, specialized layers coordinated by a centralized metadata controller.[1, 11] The stateless compute tier, known as the TiDB Server, handles incoming connections, parses and optimizes SQL queries, and generates distributed execution plans.[9, 11] Because this tier does not persist data locally, instances can be horizontally scaled behind standard load balancers to manage fluctuating concurrent connection volumes.[9, 11]The primary transactional storage tier is TiKV, a distributed, transactional key-value engine.[9, 11] Data in TiKV is logically partitioned into contiguous key-ranges called Regions, which are maintained at a default size of 96 megabytes to facilitate rapid splitting, merging, and migration across the cluster.[4, 11, 12] Each Region is replicated across multiple TiKV nodes using the Raft consensus algorithm, ensuring strong data consistency and high availability even in the event of node or data center failures.[1, 9, 11]Coordination of this decentralized network is performed by the Placement Driver (PD).[1, 11] The PD serves as the central brain of the cluster, maintaining the real-time topology of all TiKV nodes, tracking Region distribution, dynamically issuing load-balancing and Region-scheduling commands, and acting as the global logical clock for transaction sequencing.[10, 11, 12]To enforce strict ACID guarantees across distributed Regions without sacrificing horizontal scale, TiDB implements a two-phase commit (2PC) protocol based on Google's Percolator model.[1, 10, 12, 16] This transaction system relies heavily on Multi-Version Concurrency Control (MVCC) and physical-logical timestamps allocated by the PD.[10, 12, 16] When a transaction is initiated, the TiDB compute server requests a start timestamp (start_ts) from the PD, establishing the logical snapshot boundary for all subsequent read operations.[10]During execution, all mutations are buffered in the compute server's local memory.[10, 16] When the client issues a commit, the prewrite phase begins: the TiDB server selects a Primary Key from the mutation set and designates the remaining mutations as Secondary Keys.[10] It then sends parallel prewrite requests to the respective TiKV Region leaders.[10] Each TiKV node attempts to write the mutated values to a temporary lock column in its local key-value store.[10] If another concurrent transaction has already written to a key with a commit timestamp greater than start_ts, or if an unreleased lock exists on the key, a write conflict is detected, triggering a rollback or retry depending on the locking strategy.[10, 16]If all prewrites succeed, the TiDB server obtains a commit timestamp (commit_ts) from the PD and commits the Primary Key.[10] Once the primary lock is successfully released, the transaction is physically committed.[10] The remaining secondary locks are then asynchronously resolved and removed.[10]
High-Performance Analytics via the Hybrid Transactional/Analytical Processing ModelThe execution of complex analytical queries directly on transactional row-oriented storage engines introduces severe resource contention.[14, 17, 18] Massive table scans and aggregate functions starve the critical OLTP write paths of CPU cycles, memory allocations, and disk I/O bandwidth.[14, 18] TiDB resolves this conflict through its Hybrid Transactional/Analytical Processing (HTAP) architecture, which maintains physically isolated row-format and column-format replicas of the same data.[14, 18]Transactional writes are directed exclusively to the TiKV row-oriented engine, which is optimized for rapid point writes and point queries.[4, 14] Real-time analytical acceleration is provided by TiFlash, a columnar storage extension built on ClickHouse coprocessors.[14] Rather than relying on traditional replication channels or application-level ETL processes, TiFlash is integrated directly into the distributed consensus tier of the storage layer as a Raft Learner.[12, 14, 18]Because Learner replicas do not participate in the voting quorum required to commit Raft log entries, any network latency spikes, node failures, or heavy analytical processing loads on the TiFlash nodes have zero impact on the write throughput or commit latency of the TiKV transactional tier.[12, 14, 18]Raft leaders on TiKV asynchronously replicate transactional logs directly to TiFlash nodes in a many-to-many topology.[12, 14, 18] Upon receiving these serialized Raft log entries, the TiFlash proxy component extracts the row-format tuples and converts them into highly compressed, columnar-format data structures optimized for vectorized scanning and execution.[12, 13, 14]Despite replication being asynchronous, TiDB guarantees strong Snapshot Isolation consistency for all HTAP queries, ensuring that analytical queries never read stale data.[14] This consistency is achieved through a Read Index/Progress Validation protocol at read time.[14]When the TiDB compute server routes a query to a TiFlash node, it associates the query with the current read timestamp (T_{\text{read}}).[14] The TiFlash Region replica immediately sends a lightweight RPC request to the corresponding TiKV Region leader to obtain its current committed log index (the ReadIndex) corresponding to T_{\text{read}}.[14] The TiFlash node then blocks the execution of the columnar scan until its local asynchronous replication progress has applied the Raft logs up to or past that retrieved index.[14] Once the validation is complete, TiFlash performs the read, guaranteeing that the analytical client sees all writes committed prior to T_{\text{read}}.[14]
SQL-Centric Vector Retrieval and External Faiss Integration PipelinesTo support artificial intelligence workloads such as Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) within a unified database system, TiDB supports vector search through two primary mechanisms: native vector indexing within its HTAP columnar tier, and integration with the external Facebook AI Similarity Search (Faiss) library for dedicated high-speed similarity search and clustering.[15, 19, 20, 21, 22]Native Vector Storage and HNSW IndexingWithin TiDB, vector embeddings are stored using a native VECTOR data type supporting up to 16,383 dimensions.[4, 15] The physical storage and execution of these vector indexes are handled by the TiFlash columnar storage engine.[15] Consequently, before a vector search index can be built or utilized on a table, a TiFlash replica must be manually or automatically provisioned for that table.[15]Within TiFlash, data is physically partitioned into a write-optimized Delta Layer and a read-optimized Stable Layer.[15] Incoming inserts and updates are appended to the Delta Layer in real-time, which remains unindexed to maximize write ingestion speed.[15]Data in the Delta Layer is periodically merged and compacted into the Stable Layer, where the vector search index—implemented using the Hierarchical Navigable Small World (HNSW) graph-based algorithm—is built synchronously.[15] Because the index is strictly bound to the Stable Layer, any vector data lingering in the Delta Layer must be searched using exhaustive, brute-force exact matching.[15]External Faiss Integration LogicFor architectures requiring dedicated, ultra-high-speed similarity search and clustering of dense, high-dimensional vector datasets, TiDB integrates with the external Facebook AI Similarity Search (Faiss) library.[19, 20, 21] Faiss, developed by Facebook AI Research (FAIR), provides advanced clustering and nearest-neighbor search algorithms designed to scale efficiently across millions or billions of vectors, complete with native GPU acceleration.[21]In this integrated configuration, TiDB serves as the highly available, distributed relational foundation storing the source documents, structured metadata, and transaction states.[20, 22] The external Faiss library acts as a specialized query-time coprocessor.[19, 20] During operation, high-dimensional embeddings are generated by upstream machine learning models and ingested into TiDB.[23, 24]The relational records are mapped to vector embeddings, and the indexing operations are offloaded to Faiss, which structures the vectors into dedicated index files.[19, 20, 21]Faiss utilizes two primary indexing structures to balance search accuracy, build time, and memory footprint [21]:IndexFlatL2: A fundamental, brute-force index that performs exhaustive similarity searches by computing the exact L2 (Euclidean) distance between the query vector and every vector in the dataset.[21] This index guarantees 100% search accuracy but scales linearly (O(N)), making it suitable primarily for smaller, highly sensitive datasets.[21, 25]IndexIVFFlat: An Inverted File (IVF) partition-based index that uses k-means clustering to segment the high-dimensional vector space into Nlist distinct clusters, represented by centroids.[21, 26] During a search, the system computes the distance between the query vector and these centroids, restricting the downstream nearest-neighbor search to the points contained within the Nprobe closest clusters.[21, 26] This index drastically reduces the number of distance calculations, scaling search times logarithmically.[25, 26, 27]Hyperparameter Tuning in IVF IndexesTo balance recall accuracy and query latency within IVF indexes, system administrators must tune three primary hyperparameters [26]:Centroid Count (Nlist): Controls the granularity of the spatial partition.[26] A common industry rule of thumb is to set Nlist \approx \sqrt{N}, where N represents the total number of vectors in the dataset.[26] If Nlist is set too low, individual clusters become too large, forcing slower search times within clusters.[26] If set too high, the index pays an elevated cost calculating distances to many centroids.[26]Search Breadth (Nprobe): Defines the number of adjacent clusters to explore during query execution.[26] Increasing Nprobe improves search recall (approaching the accuracy of an exhaustive search) but increases query latency linearly.[26] Typically, Nprobe is set dynamically between 4 and 64 depending on the target accuracy SLA.[26]Product Quantization Subvectors (M) and Bits (K): Quantizes vectors into smaller, compressed representations, dividing a d-dimensional vector into M subvectors, each quantized using K bits (typically 8 bits, yielding 256 centroids per subspace).[26] This compression reduces the index memory footprint but introduces quantization errors that must be managed.[26]
Application Integrations, Relational Query Semantics, and Filtering OptimizationA key advantage of integrating vector search into a distributed SQL-centric environment is the ability to leverage existing relational databases, database drivers, and Object-Relational Mapping (ORM) frameworks.[1, 11, 24]Python ORM Integration FrameworksIn Python environments, developers can interact with TiDB's vector search features using standard ORM libraries like SQLAlchemy, Peewee, and Django via the django_tidb adapter.[24] These integrations allow developers to define vector fields and compile vector indexes directly within their declarative schema classes.[24]To execute a similarity search using this schema, the ORM translates Django or SQLAlchemy expressions into SQL queries containing specific distance functions and order-by clauses, mapping the high-dimensional similarity search to native SQL primitives.[15, 24]This capability enables integration into larger cognitive AI frameworks like the Dify Knowledge Pipeline.[23] Within Dify, unstructured data is automatically parsed, chunked, and embedded by upstream model nodes before being stored in TiDB Vector alongside metadata.[23] When a user queries a downstream Agent or Chatflow, the Knowledge Retrieval Node converts the incoming prompt into a vector, queries TiDB for the most semantically relevant chunks, and injects this context directly into the LLM prompt template.[23]Relational Pre-Filtering vs. Post-Filtering MechanicsWhen combining vector search with structured metadata filtering, the design of the SQL statement determines whether the database optimizer can utilize the vector search index.[15]Pre-Filtering (Disables Vector Index)A query that applies a standard relational filter before the vector distance sorting cannot utilize the HNSW index.[15]Because the WHERE clause filters out rows prior to nearest-neighbor evaluation, the pre-built HNSW graph cannot be navigated reliably.[15] The optimizer is forced to perform a full relational table scan, computing the exact vector distance for all matching rows manually.[15]Post-Filtering (Enables Vector Index)To leverage the vector index while applying metadata constraints, the query must be structured to perform the vector similarity search within an isolated subquery, followed by the metadata filter on the resulting candidate set [15]:By retrieving the top 100 nearest neighbors via the index first, the outer query can filter down the resulting candidates, maintaining sub-millisecond execution times even on tables containing millions of rows.[15]
Heterogeneous Hardware Acceleration: GPU-Enabled Matrix and Relational ProcessingWhile distributed databases scale compute horizontally by adding nodes, CPU bounds still limit performance when processing complex joins and aggregations on massive tables.[29] To address these CPU bottlenecks, TiDB incorporates heterogeneous hardware acceleration through a query execution runtime called CURA (CUDA Relational Algebra), which offloads relational processing and matrix operations directly to NVIDIA GPUs.[29]Inter-Process Communication and the Cgo BridgeTiDB is implemented in Golang, which provides excellent concurrency and network routing but lacks native support for direct GPU programming.[1, 29] Conversely, high-performance GPU execution kernels must be written in C++/CUDA.[29]To run GPU operations within the same memory space and avoid the latency of inter-process communication (IPC) or remote procedure calls (RPCs), CURA uses Cgo-based intra-process communication.[29]The Golang executor parses the SQL statement and hands a JSON-serialized CURA physical plan down to the C++ runtime.[29] This plan is compiled into CUDA-native execution kernels built on top of the RAPIDS cuDF library.[29]Columnar Alignment and Zero-ReformattingA common bottleneck in GPU-accelerated databases is the host-to-device (CPU to GPU VRAM) memory copy time.[29] If data structures must be serialized, reformatted, or rearranged before being pushed to GPU memory, the transformation overhead can negate any GPU execution speedup.[29]To solve this, CURA leverages the Apache Arrow columnar memory layout.[29] Because cuDF natively consumes Arrow-formatted columns, TiDB can transfer data fragments directly from system RAM to GPU VRAM using standard bulk memory copies (memcpy) with zero-reformatting or serialization overhead.[29]Relational Operator Offloading and Concurrent Hash TablesCURA targets the most CPU-intensive relational operators, specifically Hash Join and Hash Aggregation.[29]Highly Parallel GPU Hash Tables: Standard CPU hash joins build a sequential hash table in RAM, which is bottlenecked by single-core throughput.[29] cuDF implements highly parallel, concurrent GPU hash tables that couple open-addressing collision resolution with the massive thread parallelism of GPU architecture.[29] This GPU-accelerated hash table performs several hundred times faster than legacy CPU implementations, driving the majority of the 10x to 150x analytical speedups observed.[29]Hybrid Plan Splitting: To optimize efficiency, table scans and disk-bound operations are kept on the CPU.[29] The CURA plan represents these CPU-bound operations using placeholder nodes called InputSource nodes.[29] The CPU streams data fragments in parallel, feeding the GPU pipelines with continuous, aligned memory chunks to keep the GPU execution cores saturated.[29]Memory Pooling and Stream ManagementStandard CUDA operations like cudaMalloc and cudaFree block execution and force global device synchronization across the entire GPU.[29] To prevent these allocations from stalling query loops, CURA utilizes the RAPIDS Memory Manager (RMM) arena allocator.[29] At cluster startup, RMM pre-allocates a large contiguous block of GPU VRAM.[29]All subsequent allocations and deallocations for query execution are handled internally within this pre-allocated arena, avoiding device-wide synchronizations.[29]Additionally, CURA routes different CPU execution threads to distinct per-thread default CUDA Streams.[29] This allows the GPU to overlap independent Host-to-Device memory copies, kernel executions, and Device-to-Host memory copies, boosting overall GPU utilization.[29]This heterogeneous strategy is supported by industry studies on systems like RateupDB, a heterogeneous HTAP (H2TAP) database designed to optimize CPU, GPU, and DRAM resources to handle co-running hybrid workloads without resource starvation.[17]
Dedicated Cloud-Native Vector Platforms: Milvus and Zilliz CloudWhile consolidated SQL engines simplify database architectures, dedicated cloud-native vector databases like Milvus (and its managed SaaS counterpart, Zilliz Cloud) are engineered specifically for high-dimensional vector similarity search at multi-billion scale.[3, 5, 8] Milvus decouples compute and storage into a stateless microservices architecture, allowing organizations to scale read-heavy, write-heavy, and index-heavy workloads independently.[3, 30]Microservices and Stateless Layered ArchitectureThe Milvus architecture is divided into four independent, functional layers [3, 31]:Access Layer: Consists of stateless proxy nodes that serve as the main entry point for client requests via gRPC.[3, 30, 31] Proxies validate and preprocess requests, execute static metadata filtering, and aggregate intermediate results from worker nodes before returning the final top-K candidates to the client.[3, 31]Coordinator Services: Orchestrate load balancing, metadata management, resource scheduling, and schema definitions.[3, 31] Coordinators assign execution tasks to worker nodes based on real-time cluster health.[31]Worker Nodes: Decentralized compute units divided into three specialized execution roles [3, 31]:Data Nodes: Ingest real-time streaming updates, write incoming vectors to the Write-Ahead Log (WAL), and pack vectors into logical segments.[3, 8]Query Nodes: Load sealed index segments from cloud storage and process low-latency similarity searches.[3]Index Nodes: Build memory- or disk-optimized vector indexes (HNSW, DiskANN, IVF) on packed segments asynchronously in the background.[3, 30]Storage Layer: Implements a zero-disk, cloud-native design.[3] Persistent data, indices, and metadata are written directly to object storage (AWS S3, Google Cloud Storage, or Azure Blob Storage), eliminating the operational complexity of managing local physical disks.[3, 30, 31]Enterprise Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) Security ArchitectureFor enterprises with strict data sovereignty, privacy, and regulatory compliance requirements (such as HIPAA, GDPR, or SOC 2 Type II), Zilliz Cloud offers a Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) deployment model.[32, 33, 34] BYOC addresses the trade-off between the convenience of a fully managed SaaS platform and the security of a completely isolated, self-hosted deployment.[32, 33, 35]BYOC enforces a strict Dual-Plane Isolation Architecture [33, 34, 35]:Secure Control Plane (Zilliz Account): Deployed within Zilliz's cloud environment, the Control Plane orchestrates cluster provisioning, coordinates automated software updates, and monitors health.[33, 34, 35] Crucially, the Control Plane has zero visibility into customer data, communicating exclusively via secure outbound-only TLS 1.2+ connections on port 443.[33, 34, 35]Isolated Data Plane (Customer Account): The database execution engine—including Kubernetes clusters (EKS/GKE), EC2 instances, and S3 storage buckets—deploys directly within the customer's private cloud subscription.[33, 34, 35] All raw vector embeddings, metadata, indexes, and query flows remain entirely within the customer's secure Virtual Private Cloud (VPC).[33, 35]Infrastructure-Controlled (BYOC-I) Mode: For extreme security scenarios, BYOC-I removes Zilliz's cross-account role assumption access entirely.[33, 34, 36] Instead, a lightweight BYOC Agent is deployed locally inside the customer's Kubernetes cluster.[34, 36] The customer retains complete authority over all infrastructure resources, executing deployments and security patches via declarative code formats (such as the Zilliz Cloud Terraform Provider).[33, 36]Network Isolation and Threat Defense: Complete network isolation is established using cloud-private connections such as AWS PrivateLink.[34, 35] This eliminates any public internet exposure for database traffic.[35] Advanced threat detection and DDoS mitigation are integrated via a Web Application Firewall (WAF) integrated directly into the Network Load Balancer (NLB).[35]
Scale-Out Disk-Based Indexing via DiskANN Storage LayoutsWhile graph-based indexes like HNSW offer extremely low search latency and high recall, they are constrained by the memory wall.[25, 37, 38] Because HNSW requires the entire graph topology and all original vector data to reside permanently in RAM, indexing a dataset of one billion 768-dimensional vectors requires several terabytes of memory, rendering it cost-prohibitive for commodity systems.[38, 39]To bypass this cost limitation, Milvus implements DiskANN, a disk-based approximate nearest neighbor search algorithm designed to run massive datasets on workstation-class hardware with limited memory.[39, 40, 41]Physical Storage Layout on NVMe SSD and RAMDiskANN optimizes resource allocation by dividing index data across memory and disk tiers [38, 40]:Vamana Graph (Disk): Unlike hierarchical graphs, DiskANN builds a single, flat Vamana Graph optimized for sequential disk layout, which is stored on NVMe SSD.[38, 40]Product Quantization (PQ) Codes (RAM): Every raw vector is compressed using Product Quantization (PQ) down to a tiny, highly efficient code (e.g., 32 bytes) and stored permanently in system RAM.[38, 40]Original Raw Vectors (Disk): The uncompressed, high-dimensional original vectors are stored on disk alongside the Vamana graph edges.[38, 40]During a search query, DiskANN navigates this graph using a two-stage query pipeline [38, 40]:In-Memory Graph Traversal (The Beam-Walk Phase): The search query begins at a pre-calculated entry point—the medoid (global centroid) of the Vamana graph.[39, 40] The search algorithm traverses the graph, using a candidate list of size L (search_list) to track potential neighbors.[38, 40] Rather than executing slow disk reads to fetch raw vectors during traversal, DiskANN estimates all distances dynamically using the highly compressed PQ codes residing in RAM.[38] No disk I/O occurs during this phase.[38]Disk-Based Re-ranking Phase: Once the search has homed in on the top candidates, the query engine initiates a small number of parallel, random disk reads (typically fewer than 20) to fetch the full uncompressed vectors from the NVMe SSD.[38] It then recomputes the exact distance to resolve any quantization errors, ensuring high recall before returning the top-K results.[38, 40]To optimize DiskANN within Milvus, practitioners can adjust critical parameters inside milvus.yaml or directly via the SDK.[39, 40]PQ Code RAM Budget CalculationThe physical memory allocated for storing the compressed PQ codes in RAM is calculated using the following formulation [40]:\text{PQ Code Budget (GB)} = \text{vec_field_size_gb} \times \text{pq_code_budget_gb_ratio}Where \text{vec_field_size_gb} represents the raw uncompressed size of the vector field.[40]Beam Width Parallelism CalculationTo optimize I/O performance on fast multi-core systems, the physical number of parallel disk reads executed during re-ranking is computed as [40]:\text{Beam Width} = N_{\text{CPU}} \times \text{beam_width_ratio}Where N_{\text{CPU}} represents the number of logical CPU cores available to the execution process.[40]
FreshDiskANN and High-Throughput Streaming Similarity Search Classic DiskANN was designed for static datasets.[43] When subjected to a continuous stream of real-time insertions, deletions, and updates, static graph structures steadily degrade in search recall and navigation quality.[41] Furthermore, executing topological repairs on an active, coupled index introduces massive disk write amplification and redundant I/O bottlenecks.[44, 45] The Redundant I/O Challenge of Coupled Indexes In a traditional coupled storage index, each node's graph topology (edges list) and raw vector data are stored together inside the same physical page on disk.[45] While this layout is highly efficient for query operations (requiring only one disk access per hop), it is highly inefficient for dynamic updates [45]: Vector updates are physically lightweight, involving only modifications to the vector’s dimensions.[45] Topological updates (especially node deletions) are highly complex, requiring the removal of edges and subsequent path repairs across all incoming and outgoing neighbors of the deleted node.[45] Because vectors and edges are structurally coupled in the same physical page, any topological repair inevitably triggers massive, redundant reads and writes of the heavy raw vectors, with physical I/O operations accounting for 57.9% to 80.5% of total update times.[45] Decoupled Storage Architecture To resolve this bottleneck, FreshDiskANN physically separates the heavy, low-update raw vectors from the lightweight, high-update graph topology.[44, 45] By decoupling these layers on disk, topological repairs during insertions and deletions are executed solely on the graph topology page.[44, 45] This design reduces the total update time by 56% to 84% compared to coupled architectures, making it highly effective for streaming updates.[44, 45] While the decoupled architecture drastically improves write operations, the physical separation of topology and vector data introduces a 23% query latency penalty.[45] During search graph traversal, expanding a new node requires reading both the topology and the associated vector to calculate distances, doubling the physical page accesses.[45] To overcome this, FreshDiskANN implements a three-stage query execution and incremental reordering protocol, which optimizes page fetch sequences and improves query efficiency by 2.66x compared to traditional decoupled baselines, neutralizing the latency penalty.[45] Dual-Index Architecture and StreamingMerge To maintain real-time freshness without blocking search performance, FreshDiskANN implements a hybrid storage model [41]: Short-Term Index (TempIndex): A fast, in-memory index that buffers all incoming inserts and updates.[41] Points inside the TempIndex are instantly searchable, guaranteeing data freshness.[41, 43] Long-Term Index (LTI): The master on-disk index containing the decoupled Vamana graph on NVMe SSD.[41] StreamingMerge: Periodically, a background merge process consolidates updates from the TempIndex into the LTI.[41] Unlike standard merge operations that rebuild the entire index, StreamingMerge executes a specialized two-pass consolidation.[41] The computational and write complexity of this merge is proportional solely to the size of the incoming update set rather than the entire database, preventing I/O spikes and maintaining high system throughput.[41] Core Algorithms of FreshVamana FreshDiskANN relies on specialized algorithms to manage dynamic streaming updates while guaranteeing Quiescent Consistency (ensuring any query executed at time t reflects a total logical ordering of all modifications completed prior to t).[41]
Dynamic Insertion and Edge Pruning When a new vector p is ingested [41, 46]: The system executes a graph traversal to identify its approximate nearest neighbors, forming a candidate pool.[41, 46] Bi-directional edges are established between p and its closest neighbors.[46] If adding these connections causes any node's out-degree to exceed the physical limit R (max_degree), the system executes a relaxed robust pruning procedure (RobustPrune).[41, 46] This selects a diverse subset of neighbors based on angular distance, maintaining the navigability of the graph.[39, 41] To support thousands of concurrent updates, FreshVamana uses fine-grained, short-duration locks on the out-neighborhood N_{\text{out}}(p) of active nodes.[41] This fine-grained locking allows write throughput to scale near-linearly with the number of CPU cores.[41]
Lazy Deletion and Edge Repair Eagerly updating graph connections on disk for every individual deletion is highly resource-intensive.[41] To optimize performance, FreshDiskANN employs a Lazy Deletion strategy [41]: DeleteList Buffering: When a node p is deleted, it is not immediately removed from the physical on-disk graph.[41] Instead, its identifier is appended to an in-memory DeleteList.[41] Navigational Filtering during Search: During a query, the search algorithm is allowed to traverse through deleted nodes to reach other active areas of the graph.[41] However, any node present on the DeleteList is filtered out of the final nearest neighbor results returned to the client.[41] Parallel Batch Consolidation: Once the DeleteList accumulates a substantial volume of deletions (typically 1% to 10% of the index), a background batch process executes physical graph repair [41]: It removes all incoming and outgoing edges incident to each deleted node.[41] To prevent graph fragmentation and maintain search paths, the system connects the deleted node's in-neighbors directly to its out-neighbors by adding directed edges (p', p'') for every pair of original directed edges (p', p) and (p, p'').[41] The RobustPrune procedure is run on any updated nodes whose out-degree exceeds R.[41] This process is parallelized using prefix sums and parallel map operations to update neighborhoods locally on disk.[41]
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Architectural Synthesis and Strategic Selection MatrixTo guide infrastructure design decisions, the following matrix compares the core capabilities of consolidated distributed SQL systems and dedicated cloud-native vector databases.Strategic RecommendationsBased on this synthesis, organizations should choose their architecture according to their primary workload characteristics:Adopt Consolidated NewSQL (TiDB) if: The core application relies on complex relational schemas, strict ACID transactional integrity, and strong data consistency.[1, 9, 16] Offloading vector operations to TiDB simplifies the data stack, eliminates custom ETL sync pipelines, and allows the reuse of existing MySQL skills, ORMs, and analytics tools.[1, 4, 11, 22]Adopt Dedicated Cloud-Native Vector Platforms (Milvus/Zilliz) if: The primary workload requires scaling vector search to hundreds of millions or billions of high-dimensional embeddings.[3, 5, 31] Milvus's decoupled storage, DiskANN memory optimization, and FreshDiskANN streaming update support are designed for large-scale AI applications that require low query latencies and high write throughput.[3, 5, 38, 43]

Resource Planning FAQ: Backup and Restore FAQ: Users & Roles FAQ: Monitors & Metrics FAQ: Authentication FAQ: Integrations FAQ: Security Version: User Guides (BYOC) On this page BYOC Overview Contact Sales to Enable BYOC file_copy Copy page Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) is a deployment option for organizations to host applications and data in their own cloud accounts instead of using Zilliz Cloud's infrastructure. This solution is ideal for organizations with specific security requirements or regulatory compliance needs that require maintaining full data control sovereignty. 📘 Notes Zilliz BYOC is currently available in General Availability. For access and implementation details, please contact Zilliz Cloud support. Why use Zilliz BYOC Zilliz BYOC delivers unique deployment options that let you keep complete control over your data while eliminating operational overhead with the following benefits: Operations You can create BYOC projects and deploy the infrastructure on the Zilliz Cloud console. You can use the well-tuned metrics and alert settings for monitoring your BYOC clusters in projects. Scalability You can always scale your BYOC projects by purchasing more licenses. Clusters in BYOC projects are also scalable with manual and automatic scaling mechanisms. Data management & security Role-based access control (RBAC) at the organization, project, and cluster levels. All data is securely stored and processed within your cloud accounts. How it works BYOC involves deploying Milvus alongside Zilliz-managed backend services, such as the upgrade workflow, resource scheduler, Open API service, and the web console, within your cloud environment, typically inside your own Virtual Private Cloud (VPC). This setup ensures that your data is stored and processed within your own infrastructure. Zilliz BYOC implements two deployment modes to adapt to diversified enterprise governance requirements, and they are BYOC BYOC-I BYOC In this fully managed mode of Zilliz BYOC, you employ the cross-account role assumption mechanism offered by the cloud provider, allowing Zilliz Cloud to take on permissions to manage the EKS cluster and EC2 instances for you. According to the architecture outlined above, you need to provide a VPC, an S3 bucket, and the minimum permissions for Zilliz Cloud to start an EKS cluster and deploy the necessary components, such as the Milvus Operator, Import/Backup tools, the monitoring stack, including Grafana and Prometheus, and a Milvus instance on your behalf. Notes that the monitoring stack is integrated locally within your BYOC infrastructure, not through the Zilliz Cloud control plane. To enable and configure monitoring integrations, please contact Zilliz Technical Support. Additionally, Zilliz Cloud establishes two separate planes for communication with the components deployed in your VPC, and they are Control Plane The control plane facilitates communication between Zilliz Cloud and the components deployed in your VPC for scheduling resources, upgrading Milvus instances, and providing access to the Zilliz Cloud console and control-plane open API services. Data Plane The data plane allows communication between your applications/services and the Milvus instance deployed in your VPC, specifically for data storage and retrieval. BYOC-I This mode deploys a BYOC agent in your environment for comprehensive operation and maintenance capabilities instead of using the cross-account role assumption method in a fully managed Zilliz BYOC deployment. An encrypted point-to-point (P2P) reverse tunnel is created between the Cloud Plane and the BYOC agent to improve communication security. In the BYOC-I mode, instead of asking for cross-account permissions to manage infrastructure resources on your behalf, Zilliz leaves infrastructure management entirely in your hands, thereby enhancing data control sovereignty. However, you may choose to grant the agent the necessary permissions so that Zilliz can assist you with infrastructure management if necessary. Security assurance Zilliz Cloud ensures secure communication across network boundaries through comprehensive encryption and strict access controls. Network security Internal Traffic: Full TCP/UDP communication within the cluster security group. External Traffic: Encrypted outbound-only TCP connections on port 443 enable: Connectivity to the Zilliz control plane. Access to data sources and image repositories. Same Security Group: TCP/UDP connections allowed for intra-cluster communication. Access control Secure VPN and, just-in-time, certificate-based authentication for Zilliz engineers. Every access requires approvals and is logged for audits. The control plane monitors and collects metrics through the outbound-only TCP connections. These robust measures protect data integrity and confidentiality, ensuring secure and reliable operations in the cloud. Encryption in transit Client establish HTTPS or gRPC connections to the Zilliz Cluster. HTTPS/gRPC connections use the TLS 1.2 (or better) protocol with AES-256 (256-bit Advanced Encryption Standard ) to encrypt user data in transit. Encryption at rest The data plane of Zilliz Cloud encrypts the stored data on AWS S3 using the AES-256 (256-bit Advanced Encryption Standard ) encryption algorithm. Cost management Zilliz BYOC charges for the services you use in a BYOC project through resource management. However, you will still incur infrastructure expenses from cloud service providers, as illustrated in the following diagram. Source: Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) - Zilliz
URL: https://zilliz.com/glossary/bring-your-own-cloud-(byoc)
Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) English 日本語 한국어 Español Français Deutsch Italiano Português Русский Glossary Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) Copy page Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) Glossary page Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) Glossary page Introduction Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC ) represents an innovative IT strategy that allows organizations to leverage cloud-based software or services while maintaining full control over their data by keeping it within their own cloud environment. This approach offers greater flexibility, enhanced security, and compliance benefits by allowing organizations to manage their data privately while still benefiting from the scalability and efficiency of cloud services. While organizations can use SaaS applications, they retain control over the data plane, ensuring it remains within their environment for added security and regulatory compliance. The importance of BYOC will grow significantly with the rise of AI and data-intensive applications. Recent industry surveys indicate that while 72% of organizations are now using AI, nearly half of healthcare organizations still restrict AI adoption due to data privacy concerns. This underscores how BYOC helps organizations balance innovation with strict compliance requirements, particularly in highly regulated industries. Additionally, the concept of 'BYOC for strict data' sovereignty is crucial for experienced users with specific use cases that prioritize data control and compliance. Solutions like Zilliz Cloud, WarpStream, and BentoML can help keep data secure within a user's own Virtual Private Cloud (VPC), making it an ideal approach for those who need to ensure data remains within their private cloud environments. What is Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC)? Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) is a deployment model where organizations use Software-as-a-Service (SaaS) applications hosted by a vendor, but keep their data within their own cloud environment. This approach gives organizations full control over their data, ensuring it remains secure and compliant with internal policies and regulatory requirements, while still benefiting from the scalability and flexibility of the cloud-based SaaS applications. BYOC is particularly advantageous for organizations that need to maintain data sovereignty, ensure compliance, or require customized data storage configurations. It allows businesses to tailor their infrastructure to meet specific needs and implement stringent access controls, ensuring sensitive data is kept secure and aligned with industry regulations. Who Should Care About BYOC? BYOC is particularly relevant for enterprises requiring strict data sovereignty and compliance controls, organizations in regulated industries such as healthcare, finance, and government that must keep data within specific jurisdictions, companies needing hybrid or multi-cloud strategies to optimize performance and cost, and AI and data-driven businesses looking for better control over infrastructure costs and security. Implementation Approaches Organizations typically consider several approaches when deploying cloud services, each with distinct advantages and trade-offs: Public SaaS Solutions One common choice is adopting public SaaS solutions. These services provide robust, ready-to-use capabilities, making them an attractive option for organizations looking to accelerate deployment and minimize maintenance. However, they require storing data within vendor-managed cloud accounts, which means communications often occur over public networks. This setup can pose significant concerns for industries with strict regulatory and data sovereignty requirements, making public SaaS less viable for highly regulated sectors. When considering BYOC solutions versus SaaS, the cost comparison is complex. While BYOC requires additional infrastructure costs and operational responsibilities, it can provide better cost control and optimization opportunities in the long term, particularly for organizations with large-scale deployments. The ability to optimize infrastructure spending and avoid per-user or per month SaaS licensing fees may offset the additional operational costs for some organizations, though this varies based on scale and specific use cases. On-Premise Deployments For organizations prioritizing control, on-premises deployments offer a way to maintain full ownership over infrastructure. This approach ensures that all data remains within an organization's own environment, eliminating reliance on external cloud providers. However, this level of control comes with substantial costs. Maintaining on-prem infrastructure demands dedicated DevOps teams, leading to significant operational overhead. As technology advances, keeping systems updated and scalable can also become a considerable challenge, requiring continuous investment in both hardware and expertise. BYOC Model An alternative approach, the Bring Your Own Cloud (BYOC) model, seeks to balance the benefits of cloud-managed services with the need for infrastructure control. In this model, the vendor's software remains hosted within the SaaS environment, while the customer's data is stored within their own cloud accounts, typically in a Virtual Private Cloud (VPC). This allows organizations to leverage the scalability and efficiency of cloud computing while ensuring that their data stays within their environment. By maintaining data sovereignty, organizations can better comply with regulatory requirements without sacrificing the flexibility and operational advantages of the cloud. The BYOC model offers an adaptable deployment strategy that aligns with both security and performance needs, making it a compelling option for businesses navigating complex compliance landscapes. Core Components Control Plane The control plane serves as the orchestration center of the BYOC architecture, managed by the SaaS provider to ensure seamless operation, automation, and scalability of the application. It is responsible for managing the SaaS portion , handling authentication, authorization, and policy enforcement to ensure that access to the platform is secure and compliant with organizational and regulatory requirements. Beyond security, the control plane facilitates service orchestration and coordination , optimizing resource allocation, workload scheduling, and system configuration. Configuration management provides centralized control over application settings , ensuring consistent and automated deployments while allowing customers to adapt configurations as needed. Real-time monitoring tracks performance metrics, detects anomalies, and triggers alerts for proactive issue resolution. Additionally, the control plane offers API gateway services to streamline integration, ensuring secure and efficient API access for applications. Audit logging and compliance reporting cover activities within the SaaS environment, generating necessary documentation for governance of the platform itself. However, data governance, security, and compliance remain entirely the customer's responsibility within the data plane . The control plane ensures that the application functions securely and efficiently , while customers maintain full control over their own data infrastructure. Data Plane The data plane operates entirely within the customer's cloud environment , ensuring that all data governance, security, and compliance remain under their control . At its core, data processing engines handle computation and transformation tasks, optimizing performance for various workloads. Storage systems provide structured and unstructured data persistence, ensuring efficient data organization and accessibility. To enhance responsiveness, caching layers accelerate data retrieval, reducing latency for high-throughput applications. Scalability and reliability are achieved through load balancers , which distribute workloads to maintain high availability and prevent bottlenecks. Network security controls , including encryption and access policies, safeguard data both in transit and at rest. Backup and disaster recovery systems further ensure resilience, protecting against data loss or corruption. Since data governance is fully managed by the customer , they are responsible for implementing compliance measures, access policies, and data retention strategies within their cloud infrastructure. The data plane enables customers to leverage cloud-native scalability and flexibility , while maintaining complete control over security, governance, and regulatory requirements. Together, the control plane and data plane establish a secure, scalable, and efficient foundation for BYOC architectures , balancing SaaS automation and operational efficiency with customer control over data sovereignty and governance . Technical Architecture Infrastructure Components BYOC implements a dual-plane design that balances data sovereignty with the convenience of managed services. This architecture consists of a control plane and a data plane, each serving distinct yet complementary roles in ensuring security, efficiency, and operational control. Control Plane Architecture The control plane acts as the management layer, orchestrating key operational tasks such as resource scheduling, system upgrades, and infrastructure provisioning. It ensures efficient allocation of compute resources based on workload demands, enabling dynamic scaling without manual intervention. System upgrades are handled through automated, rolling deployments, minimizing downtime while maintaining service reliability. To enforce security, the control plane maintains secure, encrypted connections using industry-standard protocols such as TLS for API communication and end-to-end encryption for data in transit. Strict access policies, including role-based and attribute-based access controls (RBAC/ABAC), ensure that only authorized users and services can interact with critical components. Integration with identity providers like OAuth and SAML further strengthens authentication and access management. Dedicated communication channels, such as service meshes or message queues, facilitate seamless coordination between distributed services while maintaining isolation and security. By offloading these responsibilities to the SaaS provider, organizations can streamline operations, improve system resilience, and maintain a robust security posture without compromising control. Data Plane Architecture In contrast, the data plane remains fully isolated within the customer's Virtual Private Cloud (VPC), ensuring that all data processing occurs internally without reliance on shared infrastructure. Every operation, from computation to storage, is executed entirely within the customer's cloud environment, eliminating external dependencies and minimizing security risks. All data and metadata are securely contained within the VPC, with encryption applied both at rest and in transit to prevent unauthorized access. Compliance with regulatory standards such as GDPR, HIPAA, and SOC 2 is maintained through strict access controls and audit mechanisms. To further safeguard sensitive information, all communications within the data plane are encrypted using TLS, ensuring secure transmission across distributed services. This strict separation of the control and data planes allows organizations to retain full ownership and control over their information while benefiting from the scalability and automation of cloud-based services. Network Security Framework Private Endpoint Integration Private endpoints enable secure communication between the control plane and customer applications by establishing direct connections within the customer's Virtual Private Cloud (VPC). These endpoints integrate seamlessly with existing VPC security groups, ensuring that access controls align with organizational policies. Custom network ACLs (Access Control List) provide additional layers of protection, allowing fine-grained control over inbound and outbound traffic. Customers retain full management over their endpoints, enabling them to configure, monitor, and enforce security policies according to their specific requirements. Communication Security To ensure robust security, all communications are encrypted using TLS 1.2 or higher, safeguarding data during transmission. Customers have the option to manage their own certificates, enabling custom security configurations tailored to specific needs. Communication is routed through outbound port 443, which is typically used for secure web traffic, ensuring compatibility and security. Load balancing is supported with Web Application Firewall (WAF)-enabled configurations to protect against common threats and attacks. Distributed Denial of Service (DDoS) protection mechanisms are integrated to mitigate potential attacks, ensuring uptime and availability. Isolated routing policies ensure that sensitive traffic is kept separate from general communications, further reducing exposure to vulnerabilities. Additionally, granular network monitoring provides real-time insights into traffic patterns and potential security risks, allowing for prompt response and proactive management. Communication between the control plane and the data plane within a BYOC model is typically established via an IPSec tunnel or over the public internet. Security Features Access Control and Permissions Fine-Grained Permission Management Fine-grained permission management is achieved through seamless integration with cloud provider IAM (Identity and Access Management) services, allowing organizations to leverage existing security frameworks. Role-based access control (RBAC) enables the assignment of permissions based on user roles, ensuring that only authorized individuals can access specific resources or perform certain actions. Resource-level permissions provide detailed control over who can interact with specific data, services, or components. Cross-account access management allows secure collaboration across different accounts while maintaining strict access boundaries. The implementation of least-privilege access ensures that users and services are granted only the minimum permissions necessary to perform their tasks, reducing the risk of unauthorized actions and potential security breaches. It is crucial to carefully manage vendor permissions using role-based access control (RBAC) and the principle of least privilege. While vendors need specific access rights for service delivery and management, these permissions should be strictly limited to only the necessary resources and actions, with comprehensive audit logging of all vendor activities. Audit and Compliance Comprehensive Audit Logging Comprehensive audit logging ensures that all critical actions within the system are meticulously tracked for visibility and accountability. Detailed operation tracking captures every user interaction, system event, and data modification, providing a complete record of activity. Configuration changes are monitored in real-time, alerting administrators to any adjustments that could impact system security or performance. Access attempts, whether successful or unauthorized, are logged to help identify potential security risks and enforce compliance. Resource utilization monitoring tracks the consumption of compute, storage, and network resources, offering insights into system performance and optimization opportunities. Additionally, the audit logs provide robust compliance reporting capabilities, supporting audits and helping organizations meet regulatory requirements. Benefits and Advantages The solution offers a range of benefits, starting with enhanced security and compliance , ensuring data sovereignty and adherence to industry-specific regulations, such as GDPR or HIPAA. It provides greater flexibility by allowing organizations to choose and integrate their preferred cloud providers, offering more control over their infrastructure. Improved cost control is achieved by reducing reliance on expensive SaaS licenses, enabling organizations to optimize their spending. Performance optimization is another key advantage, as the solution allows businesses to fine-tune their infrastructure based on real-time workload demands, ensuring high efficiency. While BYOC requires some operational overhead for infrastructure management of their data, it combines the benefits of SaaS application management with infrastructure control. This hybrid approach allows organizations to leverage vendor expertise for application management while maintaining control over their infrastructure and data environment. Finally, the solution supports a multi-cloud strategy , facilitating seamless integration across multiple cloud providers and enhancing overall resiliency and availability. Implementation Challenges Implementing this solution presents several challenges that organizations need to consider. Integration complexity arises when connecting BYOC (Bring Your Own Cloud) infrastructure with existing IT environments, often requiring specialized expertise to ensure smooth deployment. Additionally, the increased responsibility for managing cloud infrastructure introduces operational overhead , adding to the administrative effort required to maintain and optimize the environment. This operational overhead includes the ops and security burden , which encompasses the extensive tasks of managing and securing the infrastructure. Vendor support limitations can also pose challenges, as SaaS providers may offer limited assistance for self-hosted environments, leaving organizations to troubleshoot and resolve issues independently. Lastly, security misconfigurations become a critical concern, as the responsibility for securing the data plane lies with the organization, demanding robust security policies and practices to mitigate risks. BYOC Operational Model The BYOC operational model involves a shared responsibility between the organization and the vendor. The organization is responsible for managing the cloud infrastructure, including security, maintenance, and updates, while the vendor is responsible for managing the application and providing support. This shared responsibility model requires clear communication and collaboration between the organization and the vendor to ensure seamless operations. In a BYOC model, the organization provisions the cloud infrastructure, including the virtual private cloud (VPC), and the vendor deploys the application within the VPC. The vendor may require access to the VPC for monitoring and troubleshooting purposes, but the organization retains full control over the infrastructure and data. This setup allows businesses to benefit from the vendor's expertise in application management while maintaining control over their cloud environment. The BYOC model thus offers a balanced approach, combining the advantages of self-managed software with the support and scalability of cloud-based solutions. BYOC Providers and Solutions Several vendors, including Zilliz Cloud, offer BYOC (Bring Your Own Cloud) solutions for compliance-sensitive vector search workloads. Zilliz's BYOC service enables organizations to retain full control over their data and infrastructure while leveraging the scalability and flexibility of cloud solutions. This is especially valuable for companies in regulated industries, where data sovereignty and compliance are paramount. Other vendors, like Confluent, offer a zero-access BYOC service for compliance-sensitive workloads. Confluent's solution enables organizations to keep full control over their data and infrastructure, benefiting from the scalability and flexibility of cloud services. This model is especially beneficial for organizations in regulated sectors, where maintaining data sovereignty and compliance is crucial. When selecting a BYOC provider, organizations should consider factors such as data sovereignty, access control, and security. It's essential to choose a vendor that provides a secure and reliable BYOC solution that meets the organization's specific needs and requirements. Evaluating the provider's track record, support capabilities, and compliance with industry standards can help ensure a successful BYOC implementation. Future Trends Looking ahead, several trends are set to shape the future of BYOC deployments. AI-driven automation will play a key role in reducing manual management efforts by leveraging machine learning to automate infrastructure provisioning, scaling, and optimization. Confidential computing is also emerging as a critical advancement, offering enhanced security through secure enclaves that ensure sensitive data can be processed in a trusted environment without exposure to unauthorized parties. As compliance standards continue to evolve, BYOC implementations will need to adapt to meet increasingly stringent regulatory requirements, ensuring that organizations remain compliant with new data protection and privacy laws. Best Practices By following these best practices, organizations can optimize their infrastructure, enhance security, and ensure seamless operation across their environments, paving the way for long-term success and scalability. Security Implementation To ensure robust security, organizations should implement least-privilege access principles , granting users and services only the permissions necessary for their tasks. Comprehensive audit logging is crucial for tracking user actions and system events to detect potential threats. Network isolation helps reduce the risk of unauthorized access by segregating sensitive data and workloads. It's also important to maintain up-to-date encryption standards to protect data in transit and at rest, ensuring that sensitive information is always secure. Additionally, organizations should conduct regular security assessments to identify vulnerabilities and ensure that security practices are effective and evolving to address new threats. Operational Management Efficient operational management begins with automating infrastructure deployment , reducing manual intervention and ensuring consistency across environments. Monitoring and alerting should be implemented to provide real-time insights into system performance and identify potential issues before they escalate. To safeguard against data loss, backup and disaster recovery plans must be in place and regularly tested. Performance optimization should be an ongoing effort, with regular performance reviews to fine-tune systems and infrastructure. Additionally, efficient resource scaling should be implemented to ensure that resources are dynamically allocated based on workload demands, preventing both underutilization and resource exhaustion. Multi-Cloud Considerations When adopting a multi-cloud strategy, it's essential to develop a strategy for multiple cloud provider support , ensuring that the infrastructure can seamlessly span across different platforms. Organizations should plan for cross-cloud compatibility , taking into account different architectures, services, and APIs to ensure smooth integration. Standardizing security controls across cloud platforms is critical to maintain consistent protection and compliance. Lastly, adopting a unified management approach simplifies monitoring and management across multiple clouds, ensuring centralized visibility and control over resources, security, and performance. Conclusion BYOC represents a significant shift in cloud computing strategy, offering organizations enhanced control over their infrastructure while maintaining the benefits of cloud services. While it introduces certain complexities requiring skilled management and robust architectural planning, the benefits of increased flexibility, security, and cost control make it an attractive option for many enterprises. As cloud computing continues to evolve, BYOC will play an increasingly important role in enabling scalable, secure, and compliant enterprise applications. anchor.title Introduction Implementation Approaches Core Components Technical Architecture Security Features Benefits and Advantages Implementation Challenges BYOC Operational Model BYOC Providers and Solutions Future Trends Best Practices Conclusion Start Free, Scale Easily Try the fully-managed vector database built for your GenAI applications. Building a System Where AI Searches by 'Meaning'—A Detailed Technical Explanation of Vector Databases and a Complete Guide to Product Selection｜ スネドラ 投稿 ログイン 会員登録 SYSTEM NOTICE Auto translation by AI.
