Show original Building a System Where AI Searches by 'Meaning'—A Detailed Technical Explanation of Vector Databases and a Complete Guide to Product Selection 1 スネドラ 2026年5月25日 17:53
Introduction When building a system that allows AI to read internal documents and answer questions, vector databases are unavoidable. They are the core technology supporting RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) and are responsible for determining 'which documents to retrieve.' The options have increased rapidly over the past few years. From dedicated products like Pinecone, Weaviate, and Qdrant to pgvector, which runs as an extension of PostgreSQL, the lineup of products is diverse. However, if you choose a product without understanding the underlying mechanisms, you will be helpless when you get stuck during tuning. In this article, we will delve into the mechanisms of vector databases starting from the level of indexing algorithms, compare major products as of 2026, and explain design decisions for implementation.
What is a Vector Database—Fundamental Differences from Traditional Databases 2-1. Understanding through Comparison with RDB RDBs are good at exact matches like 'WHERE name = 'Yamada Taro'' or range searches like 'WHERE price BETWEEN 1000 AND 5000'. However, this method cannot express that the expression 'a refreshing drink I want to drink in summer' is semantically connected to 'lemon soda,' 'mint iced tea,' and 'sparkling water.' Vector databases solve that problem through 'closeness in dimensional space.' They convert unstructured data such as text or images into high-dimensional numerical arrays (vectors) and perform similarity searches based on the distance in that vector space. If you search for 'cat,' it will also return 'neko,''cat,' 'nyanko,' and 'kitten.' That is the reality of semantic search. RDBs and vector databases are not conflicting concepts. In actual production systems, structured filtering such as user IDs or dates is handled by an RDB (or vector database metadata filters), while the semantic search part is handled by the vector database, allowing them to coexist. 2-2. What are Embedding Vectors The process of converting text into vectors is called Embedding. With OpenAI's text-embedding-3-small, one piece of text is converted into a 1536-dimensional vector. It is an array where 1536 floating-point numbers are lined up in a row. Embedding models are trained on massive amounts of text data and are learned to place semantically similar text at close vector coordinates and distant text at far coordinates. 'Machine learning' and 'deep learning' end up at close coordinates, while 'machine learning' and 'baseball' end up at far coordinates. There are mainly three methods for calculating the similarity between two vectors. Cosine Similaritymeasures the similarity of the direction of vectors. It is the most commonly used metric in text search and is suitable for comparing texts of different lengths because it is not affected by the vector's length (magnitude). Dot Productis the product of the magnitude and direction of the vectors. It is effective when the embedding model is optimized for dot products (such as OpenAI models) and can be calculated faster than cosine similarity. L2 Distance (Euclidean Distance) is the physical distance between coordinates and is used for image vectors or cases where the norm (magnitude) of the vector has meaning.
Indexing Technologies to Speed Up Vector Search 3-1. The Limits of Brute Force Search The simplest vector search is 'brute force' search, which calculates the distance between the query vector and all vectors in the DB and returns the closest one. It always returns an accurate answer, but it requires O(N) computational complexity for N vectors. If there are 1 million documents, 1 million distance calculations are required. Calculating 1 million 1536-dimensional vectors makes millisecond-level responses difficult to achieve straightforwardly. This is where Approximate Nearest Neighbor (ANN) search comes in. By sacrificing the guarantee of 100% recall, it achieves orders of magnitude faster performance. For most practical systems, an ANN with 95–99% recall provides sufficient quality. 3-2. IVF—Narrowing the Search Space via Clustering IVF (Inverted File Index) is a method where the vector space is pre-divided into N clusters using k-means clustering, and during a query, only the neighboring clusters are searched. The parameter nprobe controls how many clusters to examine. nprobe=1 is the fastest but reduces accuracy, while nprobe=100 increases accuracy but slows down the search. The FAISS (Facebook AI Similarity Search) library, developed by Facebook, provides an IVF implementation with a proven track record in large-scale production environments, enabling high-speed searches even with hundreds of millions of vectors. However, it has a weakness: it can overlook vectors near cluster boundaries. If a query vector is near the boundary of two clusters, the nearest neighbor will not be found if it exists in a cluster that is not being searched. 3-3. HNSW—High-Precision, High-Speed Search via Hierarchical Graph Structures HNSW (Hierarchical Navigable Small World) is a multi-layered graph structure that applies the theory of Small World Networks. It is currently adopted as the "default recommended index" in many vector databases. HNSW constructs a graph across multiple layers. The top layer is a sparse graph connecting a small number of nodes, while lower layers become denser with more nodes. During a search, you start from the top layer and navigate toward the direction of the query while descending to lower layers, allowing you to reach the vicinity of the nearest neighbor with exponentially less computation compared to a full search. It is easy to achieve over 99% recall, and node insertion and deletion can be processed in real-time. The downside is that memory consumption during index construction is higher than with IVF. There are mainly two tuning parameters. Increasing M (the number of connections per node) improves accuracy but increases memory consumption. Increasing efConstruction (the search depth during index construction) improves accuracy but increases construction time. In production, it is common to start with values around M=16 and efConstruction=200. 3-4. Quantization (PQ)—The Trade-off Between Memory and Speed Product Quantization (PQ) is a technique that significantly reduces memory usage by splitting vectors into multiple sub-vectors and replacing each sub-vector with a short code registered in a codebook. A 1536-dimensional float32 vector normally takes 1536 × 4 = 6,144 bytes (about 6KB). Applying PQ can compress this to about 16–64 bytes. Although accuracy drops slightly, it helps reduce costs in large-scale operations by keeping memory usage to less than 1/100th of the original size. HNSW+PQ, which combines HNSW and PQ, is often used in scenarios where a balance between high accuracy and low memory is required. FAISS provides this combination in forms such as IndexHNSWFlat and IndexIVFPQ, and it is widely adopted in production-grade benchmarks.
Integrated Design of RAG and Vector Databases 4-1. Basic Architecture of RAG RAG operates in two stages: the "pre-indexing phase" and the "query phase." In the pre-indexing phase, target documents are split into chunks of appropriate size, and each chunk is vectorized using an embedding model and stored in a vector database. This is performed once or whenever documents are updated. Storing metadata such as document IDs, creation dates, and categories alongside the vectors is useful for later filtered searches. In the query phase, the user's query is vectorized into the same dimensional space using an embedding model, and an ANN search is executed on the vector database to retrieve the top K similar chunks. These retrieved chunks are then incorporated into the LLM prompt as context to generate the final answer. Vector databases influence the overall performance of RAG in terms of both "index quality" and "search latency." To generate highly accurate answers, the prerequisite is to first accurately retrieve highly relevant chunks. 4-2. Guidelines for Selecting Embedding Models Here are the main embedding models considered practical as of 2026. OpenAI text-embedding-3-small (1536 dimensions) offers a good balance of cost and performance and is adopted in many RAG projects. Due to its advantages in API costs, it is one of the models with the most proven track records in commercial projects. The higher-end model, text-embedding-3-large (3072 dimensions), offers even higher precision and is an option for scenarios where quality is the top priority. Google gemini-embedding-001 is strong in multilingual support and demonstrates high precision in searches that span multiple languages, including Japanese. In the 2026 Japanese RAG benchmarks, it has shown stable top-tier performance across all language scores. ruri-v3-310m is a Japanese-specialized model that can be run locally. It is a strong option when you want to avoid dependence on commercial APIs or need to keep data on-premises. One thing to note: If you change the embedding model later, regenerating all vectors is mandatory. Because the number of dimensions and the coordinate system change when the model changes, existing vectors and new query vectors cannot be compared semantically. It is a golden rule to finalize the model before going into production. 4-3. Chunking Strategy and Its Impact The size at which you split documents directly affects the search precision of RAG. When chunks are small (128–256 tokens), it becomes easier to retrieve pinpoint information. However, because the context becomes fragmented, the quality of the vectors may decrease, and it becomes harder to retrieve information that spans multiple chunks. When chunks are large (1024–2048 tokens), the context is well-preserved, but there are cases where unnecessary information increases in the context passed to the LLM, lowering the quality of the answer. The number of chunks (K items) that can be retrieved in a single search also decreases. Generally, the range of 256–1024 tokens is widely adopted, and a standard technique is to prevent context disconnection by providing an overlap of 20–50 tokens at the beginning and end. Additionally, "Semantic Chunking," which uses paragraphs, sentences, or sections as the unit for chunking rather than a fixed length, is gaining attention as an effective method for improving precision.
Thorough Comparison of Major Vector DB Products 5-1. Overview of Major Players in 2026 As of 2026, vector DBs on the market are divided into two categories: "dedicated vector DBs" and "vector-extended DBs." Pinecone is a fully managed cloud service. You can start using it the same day with zero operations. Costs have also improved due to the serverless architecture introduced in 2024. For teams that do not want to touch infrastructure at all, there is little reason to consider other options. Query latency is generally 10–15ms, which is top-class among managed services. Weaviate stands out for its ability to execute hybrid searches (vector search + BM25 full-text search) in a single query. You can continuously adjust between pure vector search and pure keyword search with a single alpha parameter, making it easy to adapt to use cases. It also has rich enterprise-grade features and a proven track record of adoption in large organizations. Qdrant is a self-hosted vector DB implemented in Rust. Among open-source products, its cost-performance as of 2026 is outstanding, allowing you to operate millions of vectors on a small VPS for a few thousand yen per month. It is particularly excellent at maintaining low latency in searches combined with filtering, and its payload (metadata) index design is sophisticated. Chroma is a developer-oriented DB capable of local execution and in-memory operation. You can start with a few lines of Python code. It is not suitable for large-scale production environments, but if you use it for local verification or learning purposes, the introduction cost is almost zero. pgvector is an extension for PostgreSQL. If you have existing PostgreSQL infrastructure, you can introduce vector search without additional infrastructure. Since the HNSW index was added in version 0.7.0 (March 2024), its adoption in production has surged. Combined with pgvectorscale, which is described later, it is now capable of handling large-scale operations. Milvus is an open-source full-stack vector DB. It is designed for distributed processing of billions of vectors and supports multiple index types. The latest version, Milvus 2.6, also introduces 1-bit quantization (RaBitQ), which is effective for cost reduction in large-scale operations. 5-2. Product Selection Guidelines by Use Case For initial testing/PoC stage: Starting with Chroma or pgvector via Docker is the fastest route. It requires only a few lines of code and eliminates the hassle of infrastructure setup. For production environments requiring minimal infrastructure management: Pinecone is the standard choice. It offers guaranteed SLA and automatic scale-out. The ability to operate solely by managing API keys is unique. For cost-effective production environments/self-hosting preferences: As of 2026, Qdrant offers the best cost-performance ratio. Its high-performance Rust implementation achieves high throughput even on small-scale servers. For integration into existing PostgreSQL stacks: pgvector (plus pgvectorscale if needed) is the natural choice. You can manage vector data and relational data in a single database, utilizing existing SQL, ORM, and migration management ecosystems as they are.
Design Decision Points During Implementation 6-1. Scaling and Cost Design The 'number of vectors handled' is the most straightforward metric for deciding on a vector database. Here is a rough guide. ~1 million vectors: Can be handled by Chroma or pgvector's HNSW index. It is completed on a single server, and no complex operational procedures are required. 1 million to 100 million vectors: pgvector's HNSW index or Qdrant are realistic options. While appropriate index parameters and resource design are necessary, a single node can cover many use cases. Over 100 million vectors: A distributed configuration of Milvus, Weaviate, or pgvector+pgvectorscale is required. Since infrastructure costs will increase significantly, it is essential to first verify whether that scale is truly necessary. There is no need to choose a product designed for large-scale use from the start. In most cases, it is more rational in terms of initial development speed and learning costs to start with pgvector or Chroma and decide to migrate to a dedicated database only when the number of vectors and query load increase. 6-2. Utilizing Hybrid Search Vector search alone can sometimes suffer from lower accuracy in 'situations requiring exact matches.' When searching for 'Product Code AB-1234,' an exact string match is more appropriate than semantic similarity. Keyword search excels in areas where information has little variation in expression and string matching is sufficient, such as proper nouns, product names, and technical terms. Hybrid search is a technique that combines the scores of vector search (semantic search) and keyword search (full-text search like BM25). RRF (Reciprocal Rank Fusion) is often used for score integration. It is a method that integrates the ranking scores returned by both methods using reciprocal weighting, and it provides simple implementation and stable accuracy improvements. Weaviate provides Hybrid Search as a native feature, allowing you to combine vector search and BM25 in a single API call. pgvector can also implement hybrid search by combining it with PostgreSQL's full-text search feature. In many use cases, this improves recall compared to vector search alone, making it worth incorporating into production RAG systems.
Latest Trends in 2026 and Future Outlook 7-1. The Rapid Growth of pgvector—Returning from Dedicated DBs to Extended RDBs Among the changes that occurred between 2024 and 2025, one of the most significant is the rapid practical adoption of pgvector. The HNSW index was added in version 0.7.0 (March 2024), resolving a major barrier to production operation. Version 0.8.2 is currently available as the stable release and is enabled by default in major managed PostgreSQL services such as AWS RDS, Google Cloud SQL, Supabase, and Neon. Furthermore, pgvectorscale (provided by Timescale), which appeared in 2025–2026, has dramatically improved pgvector's search performance. It achieved 471 QPS while maintaining 99% recall in benchmarks with 50M vectors, which is said to be equivalent to 11.4 times the performance of Qdrant in the same test. The approach of 'starting with pgvector and migrating to a dedicated database when necessary' is becoming established as a realistic best practice for 2026. There is no longer a need to take on the complexity of building and operating a separate dedicated vector database from the start. 7-2. Evolution of Quantization Technology: How 1-bit Quantization Changes the Cost Structure Another trend directly linked to large-scale operational costs is the evolution of quantization technology. From late 2025 to 2026, 1-bit quantization (Binary Quantization) reached a practical level. The RaBitQ (Randomized Binary Quantization) technology introduced in Milvus 2.6 reduces memory usage by 72% while increasing search throughput (QPS) by four times in tests with 768 dimensions and 1 million vectors. Compressing from float32 (32-bit) to 1-bit naturally involves information loss. RaBitQ uses correction terms to compensate for that loss, minimizing practical accuracy degradation. If 1-bit quantization becomes widespread, the number of vectors that can be handled on the same hardware will increase significantly, changing the cost structure for managing hundreds of millions of vectors. The proposition of 'saving memory while maintaining accuracy' has been a long-standing challenge in large-scale vector search, but the development of quantization technology is steadily bringing us closer to a solution.
Conclusion In the last two to three years, vector databases have changed from 'something for interested people to try' to 'standard components of AI systems.' If you are building RAG, they are an essential choice. Understanding index algorithms like HNSW, IVF, and PQ will help you make faster decisions during tuning. When recall is lower than expected, latency is high, or memory consumption exceeds expectations—knowing the characteristics of each algorithm will give you a clue as to which parameters to adjust. For product selection, it is realistic to start with pgvector and migrate to a dedicated database once performance requirements become clear. Rather than setting up a complex dedicated infrastructure from the start, creating a working environment at hand quickly will lead to deeper learning. The vector database market is still moving fast. With the rise of pgvectorscale and advancements in quantization technology, it is not uncommon for the common knowledge of a year ago to be overturned. I hope you will use the content of this article as a starting point to continue following the latest trends while actually working with the technology.
